Title: Offline-Online Reinforcement Learning for Linear Mixture MDPs

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.11994v1

Published Time: Wed, 15 Apr 2026 00:08:38 GMT

Number of Pages: 72

Markdown Content:
# Offline–Online Reinforcement Learning for Linear Mixture MDPs 

Zhongjun Zhang 

> Department of Industrial Engineering and Management Sciences, Northwestern University, zhongjun@u.northwestern.edu

Sean R. Sinclair 

> Department of Industrial Engineering and Management Sciences, Northwestern University, sean.sinclair@northwestern.edu

We study offline–online reinforcement learning in linear mixture Markov decision processes (MDPs) under environment shift. In the offline phase, data are collected by an unknown behavior policy and may come from a mismatched environment, while in the online phase the learner interacts with the target environment. We propose an algorithm that adaptively leverages offline data. When the offline data are informative, either due to sufficient coverage or small environment shift, the algorithm provably improves over purely online learning. When the offline data are uninformative, it safely ignores them and matches the online-only performance. We establish regret upper bounds that explicitly characterize when offline data are beneficial, together with nearly matching lower bounds. Numerical experiments further corroborate our theoretical findings. 

Key words : Reinforcement learning, Linear Mixture MDP, Offline learning, Online learning 

## 1. Introduction 

Recent progress in reinforcement learning (RL) has demonstrated strong performance in complex domains such as large-scale video games (Silver et al. 2016, Mnih et al. 2015, Vinyals et al. 2017). However, these successes rely heavily on massive amounts of online interaction, often enabled by high-fidelity simulators. In many real-world applications, such interaction is limited or simulators are unavailable. For example, in inventory planning for new products, decisions must be made under uncertain demand without sufficient historical data to accurately simulate future outcomes, making it difficult to optimize policies through online experimentation alone. A natural approach is to leverage pre-collected data (i.e., offline data) from related environments. In retail, for instance, historical data from existing products can inform decisions for new products with limited data (Amazon.com 2022). Such transfer has proven effective in practice, particularly in cold-start settings where direct data collection is costly or infeasible (Amazon Web Services 2021). Despite its promise, leveraging offline data is challenging due to two fundamental issues. First, offline data may be generated under a similar but not identical environment, leading to bias under environment shift (Mendonca et al. 2020). For instance, historical sales from existing products may be similar but not identical to the demand distribution of a new product. Second, the offline data is collected under a behavior policy, which shapes the observed offline data that may not align with the target objective (Jin et al. 2021).   

> 1
> arXiv:2604.11994v1 [cs.LG] 13 Apr 2026 Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 2

While both purely online and offline RL are well understood for many canonical models, such as bandits and tabular MDPs, their combination (i.e., offline–online learning) remains less explored. In particular, existing work on offline–online learning often relies on simplifying assumptions on either environment shift or the offline behavior policy. Some works assume no environment shift (Hao et al. 2023, Wagenmaker and Pacchiano 2023), while others impose restrictive assumptions on the offline behavior policy, such as fixed sampling in bandits (Cheung and Lyu 2024) or uniform visitation in tabular MDPs (Chen et al. 2022). These assumptions obscure the joint role of environment shift and the behavior policy, and do not extend naturally to large or continuous state–action spaces. Linear mixture MDPs provide a natural framework to study this gap, capturing rich function approximation while retaining tractable structure (Chen et al. 2021, Zhou et al. 2021). Notably, the inventory example above can be modeled as a special case of the linear mixture MDP studied in this work (Wan et al. 2024). Addressing these challenges is nontrivial. Naively combining offline and online data without accounting for environment shift can introduce bias and lead to linear regret (Song et al. 2022). This motivates the need for algorithms that safely incorporate offline data — achieving improved regret when the offline data is infor-mative, while matching purely online guarantees otherwise. At the same time, the presence of environment shift complicates the coverage conditions and pessimism-based estimation techniques commonly used in offline RL (Jin et al. 2021, Yin et al. 2022, Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023), suggesting that these principles must be reconsidered in the offline–online setting. Motivated by these challenges, we investigate the following questions in the offline–online linear mixture MDP setting: 

How can offline data be safely leveraged in online learning under environment shift without degrading regret guarantees? How do environment shift and the offline behavior policy jointly govern the informativeness of offline data? 

1.1. Our Contributions Offline–Online Learning Algorithm to Safely Leverage Offline Data. Our first contribution is an algorithm, O-O UCRL -VTR (Algorithm 1), for offline–online linear mixture MDPs under environment shift and uncontrollable offline behavior policy. The backbone of O-O UCRL -VTR follows the UCRL with value-targeted regression ( UCRL -VTR ) algorithm in Jia et al. (2020). The key difference is that, at each stage of the online phase, we maintain two estimators of the true parameter 𝜃 ∗ that governs the transition dynamics of the online target environment: an online-only estimator based solely on online interactions, and an offline–online estimator that combines both offline data and online interactions. Our algorithm adaptively relies on the more reliable estimator, thereby leveraging offline data only when it is informative. A detailed description of O-O UCRL -VTR is provided in Section 3. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 3

We establish a regret upper bound for O-O UCRL -VTR in Theorem 1, which depends on a quantity 𝜏 

that measures the quality of the offline behavior policy. 𝜏 is defined by the minimum eigenvalue of the offline design matrix (see Definition 2), and is closely related to uniform coverage conditions in offline RL literature (Yin et al. 2022). In line with prior approaches in offline RL (Yin et al. 2022, Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023), the value of 𝜏 is not required by the implementation of O-O UCRL -VTR . The regret bound in Theorem 1 exhibits a “min-of-two” structure: it is always safeguarded by the regret of the purely online algorithm (Jia et al. 2020), and can be strictly smaller when the offline data is informative, due to either small environment shift or large coverage 𝜏 .

Characterization of Offline Data Informativeness and Coverage 𝜏 . In contrast to the nearly minimax regret in the purely online setting, which scales as √𝐾 with 𝐾 denoting the online interaction budget (Jia et al. 2020), we provide, in Section 4.2, sufficient conditions under which offline data is informative, that is, yields 𝑜 (√𝐾 ) regret (see Definition 3) in the online learning process. We first consider the “sufficient coverage” case 𝜏 = Θ (1). In this regime, offline data is informative if: (i) the offline sample size 𝑀 off is sufficiently large relative to the online interaction budget 𝐾 ; and (ii) the environment shift Δ is below a threshold determined by 𝐾 , where Δ is measured in the ∥ · ∥ 2 norm (see Assumption 1). This aligns with the intuition that more offline data and smaller environment shift, when properly leveraged as in Algorithm 1, lead to greater offline data informativeness. We further show in Section 4.3 that 𝜏 = Θ (1) holds under the conventional uniform coverage condition in the offline RL literature (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023), as formalized in Assumption 2, under appropriate design of offline value function estimates (see Corollary 2 and Corollary 3). Notably, the offline value function estimates we propose in Section 4.3 differ from conventional pessimism-based principles in the offline RL literature, as the optimal policies in the offline and online environments can differ substantially due to environment shift. We next consider the complementary case 𝜏 = 𝑜 (1). In this case, offline data is informative if: (i) the coverage 𝜏 exceeds a threshold determined by 𝐾 and 𝑀 off ; and (ii) the ratio Δ/𝜏 is below a threshold determined by 𝐾 . Condition (ii) implies a linear relationship between Δ and 𝜏 , so that larger 𝜏 permits proportionally larger environment shift while preserving offline data informativeness. This regime captures a less explored setting in the offline RL literature, where the offline behavior policy is adaptive (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023). We provide a bandit example in Proposition 2 showing that offline data can remain informative when the behavior policy follows UCB1 (Auer et al. 2002). In this case, uniform coverage (Assumption 2) is violated, yet our algorithm achieves 𝑜 (√𝐾 ) regret under suitable regimes of 𝐾 , 𝑀 off , and Δ.

Nearly Matching Regret Lower Bound. We also provide a nearly matching lower bound in Section 5. The lower bound considers the case when 𝜏 = Θ (1) and exhibits a similar “min-of-two” structure, and nearly match the upper bound in Theorem 1 in terms of 𝑑, 𝑀 off , 𝐾 and Δ, in both regimes where offline data is informative and where it is not. A discussion of the regret lower bound for the complementary case 𝜏 = 𝑜 (1)

is also provided in Section 5. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 4

Empirical Simulations. We conduct empirical simulations in a synthetic setting, comparing O-O UCRL -

VTR with several baseline algorithms for linear mixture MDPs in Section 6. The simulation results corrob-orate our analysis: (i) larger values of 𝜏 and smaller environment shift Δ lead to greater informativeness of the offline data; (ii) O-O UCRL -VTR is robust, performing no worse than the purely online baseline UCRL 

when Δ is large, and significantly better when Δ is small; (iii) when Δ scales linearly with 𝜏 , offline data remains informative as Δ increases, whereas the benefit of offline data rapidly diminishes when Δ grows superlinearly than 𝜏 . We also compare O-O UCRL -VTR with more baselines designed for tabular MDPs; additional details are provided in Appendix E. 

Paper Organization. We review the related literature in the remainder of this section. In Section 2, we introduce the offline–online linear mixture MDP model, followed by a detailed description of our algorithm O-O UCRL -VTR in Section 3. Our main algorithmic results are presented in Section 4. Section 4.1 establishes the regret upper bound, and in Section 4.2, we analyze how the informativeness of offline data depends on the environment shift Δ and coverage 𝜏 . In Section 4.3, we further study how 𝜏 is shaped by the feature mapping of linear mixture MDP model, the offline behavior policy, and the design of offline value function estimates. We complement these results with nearly matching lower bounds in Section 5. Finally, we demonstrate the strong empirical performance of our algorithm in Section 6 and conclude in Section 7. 

1.2. Related Literature 

Our work is highly related to three threads of research in the literature: online RL, offline RL, and offline– online RL under either environment or distribution shift. For additional background, we refer readers to Sutton et al. (1998), Bertsekas (2019) for online RL, and to Chapter 4 of Szepesv´ ari (2022) as well as Jiang and Xie (2025) for offline RL. 

Online RL. A substantial body of work in reinforcement learning focuses on the online setting. The classical formulation is tabular RL, where both the state and action spaces are finite. Two main algorithmic paradigms dominate this literature: model-based methods (Brafman and Tennenholtz 2002, Azar et al. 2017, Auer et al. 2008, Dann et al. 2017, Simchowitz and Jamieson 2019) and 𝑄 -learning-based approaches (Jin et al. 2018). Beyond these frameworks, alternative perspectives have also been explored, including posterior sampling (Agrawal and Jia 2017) and policy-level optimization under structural assumptions on the policy class, such as convexity or counterfactual estimability (Agrawal and Jia 2022, Zhang et al. 2025b). Beyond the tabular regime, increasing attention has been devoted to online RL with function approximation in large or continuous state and action spaces. Under linear realizability assumptions, much of the recent progress concerns linear MDPs (Yang and Wang 2019, Jin et al. 2020, Zanette et al. 2020a), linear mixture MDPs (Modi et al. 2020, Jia et al. 2020, Zhou et al. 2021, Ayoub et al. 2020), and analyses based on controlling Bellman errors (Jiang et al. 2017, Zanette et al. 2020b). There is also a growing literature addressing function Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 5

approximation beyond linear models, including bilinear and more general representations (Du et al. 2021, Foster et al. 2021, Zhu et al. 2023). This work builds on the framework of online RL for linear mixture MDPs, with an explicit incorporation of an offline data phase, in which both environment shift and offline data coverage play essential roles. In the absence of offline data, our regret bound reduces to ˜𝑂 (𝑑𝐻 2√𝐾 ), matching the guarantees in Jia et al. (2020), Ayoub et al. (2020). A tighter bound of ˜𝑂 (𝑑𝐻 3/2√𝐾 ) is obtained by Zhou et al. (2021) via a Bernstein-style concentration argument, at the cost of a substantially more involved analysis. While similar techniques could potentially be used to remove the extra √𝐻 factor in our bound, our focus here is on isolating the roles of environment shift and coverage in the offline–online setting. We leave such refinements to future work. 

Offline RL. Unlike online RL, in offline RL the learner cannot interact with the environment and must rely entirely on a fixed, pre-collected dataset. As a result, coverage plays a central role in determining the statistical feasibility of offline learning. Early work focused on uniform coverage, requiring the logged data to adequately explore the entire state–action space, including Munos and Szepesv´ ari (2008) under finite pseudo-dimension assumptions and Chen and Jiang (2019) in the tabular setting. More recent studies shift to 

partial coverage, examining whether the behavior policy sufficiently covers the optimal policy’s state–action distribution. Representative works include Jin et al. (2021) and Xie et al. (2021a), which develop general function approximation frameworks with linear instantiations; Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023), which study linear MDPs and linear mixture MDPs; and Jiang and Xie (2025). In structured models such as linear MDPs and linear mixture MDPs, coverage is often characterized in the feature space via eigenvalue conditions on the design matrix, as in Yin et al. (2022) for linear MDPs and Uehara and Sun (2021), which provides a general framework instantiated in tabular, linear mixture, and low-rank MDPs. Among the works discussed above, those most closely related to our setting, namely linear mixture MDPs with feature-space coverage conditions, are Yin et al. (2022), Uehara and Sun (2021) and Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023). Yin et al. (2022) study linear MDPs under a uniform coverage assumption in the feature space, formalized via a uniformly positive minimum eigenvalue of the design matrix. This condition is closely related to our assumption (see Definition 2), though their setting focuses on linear MDPs rather than the linear mixture MDPs considered here. Uehara and Sun (2021) and Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023) consider offline RL for linear mixture MDPs under partial coverage, with the former focusing on data-space coverage and the latter on feature-space coverage. A detailed comparison between Definition 2 and the coverage condition in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023) is given in Section A.3 and Section B.3.2. All of these works achieve an suboptimality gap of order ˜𝑂 (1/√𝑀 off ), where 𝑀 off denotes the offline sample size. This rate is recovered as a special case of our results when 𝐾 = 1 and the environment shift is negligible (see Section 4.3). Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 6

Offline–Online RL. Building on the extensive literature on online and offline RL, offline–online RL has recently emerged as an active research direction. The central question is whether and how logged offline data can be leveraged to improve subsequent online learning. Beyond data coverage, which is critical in the offline phase, environment shift also becomes a key factor. When there is no environment shift and the offline and online environments coincide, it is relatively well understood in both RL and bandits that offline data can be leveraged to reduce both the bias and the uncertainty in value and model estimation (Hao et al. 2023, Wagenmaker and Pacchiano 2023, Shivaswamy and Joachims 2012, Banerjee et al. 2022, Agrawal et al. 2023, Sentenac et al. 2025, Song et al. 2022). When environment shift is present, however, the offline and online environments may differ, and this is precisely the regime studied in this work. In the bandit setting, Cheung and Lyu (2024), Yang et al. (2025) analyze offline–online learning under environment shift, but assume fixed numbers of offline pulls for each arm, so the offline behavior policy and coverage do not play an explicit role. In tabular RL, Chen et al. (2022) study the offline–online setting, but their algorithm can perform worse than a purely online method when the environment shift Δ is large, and neither the offline behavior policy nor coverage is explicitly characterized. In contrast, our work extends to linear mixture MDPs, which subsume both bandits and tabular MDPs (see Appendix A), and proposes an algorithm (Algorithm 1) whose performance is always safeguarded by a purely online baseline. Moreover, we provide a detailed characterization of how environment shift and coverage interact to determine when offline data are informative (see Section 4.2). Related lines of work also include settings where the offline behavior policy can be queried during the online phase rather than relying on logged data (Ross and Bagnell 2012, Xie et al. 2021b), as well as non-stationary RL and change-point detection (Xie et al. 2025, Auer et al. 2019). There is also a growing body of empirical studies on offline–online RL (Ball et al. 2023, Nakamoto et al. 2023, Zheng et al. 2023). Specifically, Zheng et al. (2023) study an offline–online RL setting and propose an algorithm that combines pessimism-based estimation in the offline phase with optimistic exploration in the online phase. In contrast, our analysis in Section 4, together with the simulations in Section 6, suggests that in our setting, offline data can be utilized more effectively by prioritizing statistical efficiency in the offline phase, rather than enforcing pessimism.

The smallest and largest eigenvalues of 𝐴 are denoted by 𝜆 min ( 𝐴 ) and 𝜆 max ( 𝐴 ), respectively. Offline–Online MDP. We study a reinforcement learning problem consisting of two phases: an offline phase and an online phase. Each phase is modeled as an episodic Markov decision process (MDP) with a finite horizon. Specifically, the offline MDP is given by the tuple (S , A, 𝑃 off , 𝑟 off , 𝐻, 𝑠 off 1 ), where S is the state space, A is the action space, 𝑃 off is the transition kernel, 𝑟 off : S × A → [ 0, 1] is a deterministic reward function, 𝐻 is the episode length, and 𝑠 off 1 is the initial state. The online MDP is defined analogously by 

(S , A, 𝑃, 𝑟, 𝐻, 𝑠 1). For simplicity, we assume that the state space S, action space A, and horizon 𝐻 are identical in the offline and online MDPs. For notational simplicity in what follows, we refer to the pair 

{(S , A, 𝑃 off , 𝑟 off , 𝐻, 𝑠 off 1 ), (S , A, 𝑃, 𝑟, 𝐻, 𝑠 1)} as the corresponding offline–online MDP .Throughout the paper, we restrict attention to homogeneous linear mixture MDPs (Jia et al. 2020, Zhou et al. 2021, Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023), defined as follows. 

Definition 1. There exist unknown parameter vectors 𝜃 off ,∗, 𝜃 ∗ ∈ R𝑑 such that, for all 𝑠, 𝑠 ′ ∈ S and 𝑎 ∈ A ,

𝑃 off (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = ⟨𝜃 off ,∗, 𝜙 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )⟩ , 𝑃 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = ⟨𝜃 ∗, 𝜙 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )⟩ , (1) where 𝜙 : S × A × S → R𝑑 is a known feature mapping. We assume that the feature mapping satisfies Í𝑠 ′ |𝜙 𝑗 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )| ≤ 1, for all (𝑠, 𝑎 ) and 𝑗 ∈ [ 𝑑 ], where 𝜙 (𝑠 ′ |

𝑠, 𝑎 ) = [𝜙 1 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ), . . . , 𝜙 𝑑 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )] ⊤. Moreover, we assume that max {∥ 𝜃 off ,∗ ∥2, ∥𝜃 ∗ ∥2} ≤ 𝐵, for a known constant 𝐵 > 0 (Jia et al. 2020). For future use, we define 𝜙 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = [𝜙 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )] 𝑠 ′ ∈ S ∈ R𝑑 × | S | and, for each 𝑗 ∈ [ 𝑑 ], 𝜙 𝑗 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = [𝜙 𝑗 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 )] 𝑠 ′ ∈ S ∈ R| S | .For clarity of exposition, 1 we additionally assume that the reward functions 𝑟 off and 𝑟 are deterministic and known (Jia et al. 2020, Zhou et al. 2021), and that the feature mapping 𝜙 is shared between the offline and online MDPs. We emphasize that we do not require each 𝜙 𝑗 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) to define a valid probability transition model for all (𝑠, 𝑎 ); in particular, 𝜙 𝑗 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) need not be nonnegative nor sum to one. 

Remark 1. Note that any tabular MDP can be expressed as a linear mixture MDP with dimension 

𝑑 = |S| 2 |A| . To see this, we let 𝜙 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 𝑒 (𝑠 ′ ,𝑠,𝑎 ) be a one-hot encoding of the tuple (𝑠 ′, 𝑠, 𝑎 ). Then if 𝜃 (𝑠 ′ ,𝑠,𝑎 ) = 𝑃 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) we trivially have that 𝑃 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = 𝜃 ⊤𝜙 (𝑠 ′ | 𝑠, 𝑎 ). This construction shows that the linear mixture MDP model strictly generalizes the tabular setting. We later discuss how the results in Section 4 go beyond existing guarantees for tabular MDPs (Chen et al. 2022). Finally, we assume that the discrepancy between the offline and online transition parameters is known and bounded. 

Assumption 1. ∥𝜃 ∗ − 𝜃 off ,∗ ∥2 ≤ Δ, where Δ ≥ 0 is a known constant. 

This assumption is standard in offline–online learning settings (Crammer et al. 2008, Chen et al. 2022, Besbes et al. 2022, Cheung and Lyu 2024). A detailed discussion of Assumption 1, including comparisons with environment shift assumptions in the existing literature, is deferred to Appendix A.

Online Interaction and Regret. After observing the offline dataset Doff , the learner interacts with the online MDP for 𝐾 episodes, each of horizon 𝐻 , starting from the designated initial state 𝑠 1. where the expectation is taken over the randomness of both the offline data generation and the online interaction, since the offline dataset Doff influences the deployed policies {𝜋 𝑘 }𝐾 𝑘 =1 as well. We emphasize that the offline environment may differ from the online environment. As a result, offline trajectories are, in general, biased with respect to the online dynamics. A central goal of this work is to understand when and how such biased offline data can still be exploited for online learning, and to quantify the respective effects of environment shift (i.e., 𝑃 off ≠ 𝑃 so Δ > 0) and the coverage induced by the offline behavior policy (Jiang and Xie 2025) on the resulting performance guarantees. 

## 3. Algorithm Design 

This section presents the details of Algorithm 1, which addresses offline–online linear mixture MDPs where the offline environment may differ from the online one. At a high level, the algorithm consists of two components: estimating the transition parameter 𝜃 ∗, and using this estimate to compute an optimistic approximation of the optimal value function 𝑉 𝜋 ∗ 

> 1

and the corresponding policy 𝜋 ∗. A central feature of the offline–online setting is that 𝜃 ∗ can be estimated in two distinct ways. The first is an online-only estimator that uses data collected from online interaction alone, while the second is an offline–online estimator that aggregates both offline and online data. By appropriately leveraging these two estimators, the algorithm effectively uses the more reliable estimate at each stage, enabling it to benefit from informative offline data while remaining robust when such data are uninformative.

(5)). 

## 4. Regret Upper Bound and Characterization of Offline Data Informativeness 

In this section, we first derive the regret upper bound, Theorem 1, for offline–online linear mixture MDP in Section 4.1. Building on the regret bound, we characterize regimes in which offline data is informative in Section 4.2, and analyze how the regret depends on the feature mapping 𝜙 of the offline MDP, the offline behavior policy, and the design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } in Section 4.3. We also compare Theorem 1 with prior regret bounds in the literature in Appendix A.

The regret bound has a “min-of-two” form. The first term corresponds to the offline–online estimator (term ( 𝐴 )), while the second term (term (𝐵 )) corresponds to the online-only estimator. The proof is provided in Section C.1. 

4.2. When is Offline Data Informative 

The regret bound in Theorem 1 is always upper bounded by term (𝐵 ), ˜𝑂 (𝐵𝐻 2𝑑 √𝐾 ), which matches the online-only regret guarantee for linear mixture MDPs (Jia et al. 2020). This highlights the robustness of our Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 14 

algorithm. When the offline data are uninformative (due to either poor coverage 𝜏 or a large environment shift Δ), the algorithm provably performs no worse than ignoring offline data altogether. We next characterize regimes in which offline data is informative and yields a strict improvement over the online-only baseline. 

Definition 3. Given 𝜖 ∈ ( 0, 12 ], we say that the offline data is informative with respect to 𝜖 if the regret satisfies Regret (𝐾 ) = ˜𝑂 



𝐵𝐻 2𝑑𝐾 12 − 𝜖 



.

Any such improvement must be realized through term ( 𝐴 ). The contribution of offline data improves monotonically as the environment shift Δ decreases and the coverage parameter 𝜏 increases. If no nontrivial lower bound on 𝜏 can be established, namely when 𝜏 ≤ 𝜆 /𝑀 off , then offline data becomes uninformative once 𝐾 is sufficiently large. This shows that nontrivial coverage is a necessary condition for offline data to be informative. The following corollary gives a sufficient condition, stated in terms of 𝐾 , 𝑀 off , 𝜏 , and Δ,under which offline data is informative. 

Corollary 1. If there exists 𝜖 ∈ ( 0, 12 ] such that 

𝜏 = Ω

, (14) 

then the offline data is informative with respect to 𝜖 .

The proof is deferred to Section C.2. We next discuss how the coverage parameter 𝜏 and the environment shift Δ affect the regret bound, treating 𝐾 and 𝑀 off as fixed. 

Case 1: 𝜏 = Θ (1), or equivalently, 𝜆 min (𝐺 off ) = Ω (𝑀 off ) with high probability. This corresponds to the ideal regime in which the offline data exhibit uniform coverage over the entire state–action space. In this case, Corollary 1 simplifies to the conditions 𝑀 off ≳ 𝐾 1+2𝜖 and Δ ≲ 𝐾 −1/2− 𝜖 , up to constant factors. That is, when the amount of offline data is sufficiently large relative to 𝐾 , and the environment shift Δ is suitably small, the offline data become informative. A noteworthy implication is that offline data need not be excessively large to yield substantial gains. For any 𝜖 ∈ ( 0, 12 ], when 𝑀 off = Θ (𝐾 2), whether offline data is informative depends solely on Δ. In particular, under a sufficiently small shift, e.g., Δ ≲ 𝐾 −1, the regret reduces to 𝑂 (log (𝐾 )) . This improvement indicates that, at an appropriate scale, informative offline data can substantially reduce uncertainty about the online environment before interaction begins. 

Case 2: Decaying Coverage 𝜏 as 𝑀 off Increases. Unlike Case 1, where the coverage parameter 𝜏 is assumed to be bounded away from 0, we now consider regimes in which 𝜏 may decay as 𝑀 off increases. Such behavior can arise when the offline behavior policy is adaptive , meaning that it depends on previously collected data. As 𝑀 off grows, the policy may concentrate more heavily on certain state–action pairs and reduce visits to others, thereby decreasing the effective coverage parameter 𝜏 . See Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023), Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 15 

Jin et al. (2020) for further discussion on adaptivity in offline RL. We next analyze how the scaling of 𝜏 with 

𝑀 off affects the informativeness of the offline data. The first condition in Corollary 1, 𝜏 = Ω (𝐻 3𝐾 1+2𝜖 /𝑀 off ) imposes a minimum coverage requirement. Given 𝐾 and 𝑀 off , the effective coverage 𝜏 must exceed a threshold for the offline data to be informative. The second condition, Δ/𝜏 = 𝑂 (𝐵  𝐻 7/2𝑑 1/2𝐾 1/2+𝜖  −1), shows that the admissible magnitude of the environment shift Δ scales linearly with 𝜏 . Together, these sufficient conditions indicate that stronger effective coverage (i.e., a slower decay of 𝜏 with respect to 𝑀 off ) linearly enlarges the range of environment shifts under which offline data is informative. A similar argument suggests an upper bound on the offline data size 𝑀 off that is sufficient to attain a ˜𝑂 (log (𝐾 )) regret, analogous to Case 1. We omit the details for brevity. The discussion above is summarized visually in Figure 1. 

4.3. Dependence of 𝜏 on the Feature Mapping 𝜙 , the Offline Behavior Policy, and the Design of 

{e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }

The guarantee in Theorem 1 critically depends on the coverage parameter 𝜏 . In this subsection, we study how 𝜏 is shaped by three components: the feature mapping 𝜙 , the offline behavior policy, and the design of 

{e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. The feature mapping 𝜙 determines whether a nontrivial lower bound on 𝜏 is possible, the offline behavior policy determines which state–action pairs are visited in the offline data, and the design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }

determines how these visits translate into the offline design matrix 𝐺 off .We first study a necessary condition on 𝜙 . We then consider the canonical uniform data coverage assump-tion on the offline behavior policy, under which we discuss the designs of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } and derive the corresponding lower bounds on 𝜏 . Finally, we discuss adaptive offline behavior policies, under which 𝜏 may decay with 

𝑀 off . For clarity of exposition, we assume throughout this subsection that the state and action spaces are finite. The extension to continuous spaces is similar and thus omitted. 

The Feature Mapping 𝜙 . We begin by isolating a structural property of the feature mapping 𝜙 that is necessary for 𝜏 to admit a nontrivial lower bound. Recall that the feature mapping 𝜙 of the offline MDP is assumed to be known to the learner. We say that the feature mapping of the offline MDP is uniformly learnable if the following condition holds. The term “uniformly” refers to the fact that, in Definition 2, the informativeness of the offline data is measured via a minimum eigenvalue condition over the entire feature space. 

Definition 4. The feature mapping 𝜙 of the offline MDP is uniformly learnable if there exist 𝐿 probe vectors {𝑉 1, . . . , 𝑉 𝐿 } with 𝑉 𝑙 ∈ [− 1, 1] | S | for all 𝑙 ∈ [ 𝐿 ], and state–action pairs {( 𝑠 𝑖 , 𝑎 𝑖 )} 𝐼 𝑖 =1, such that

Uniform learnability is a property of the feature mapping of the offline MDP alone and does not depend on the behavior policy or the choice of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. It formalizes the requirement that the feature mapping 𝜙 

admits sufficiently diverse directions under suitable probing. The following result shows that this condition is necessary for the offline data to be informative. 

Proposition 1. For a given offline–online linear mixture MDP, if the feature mapping of the offline MDP is not uniformly learnable, then for any behavior policy and any design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }, we have 𝜏 (𝛿 ) = 𝜆 𝑀 off for any 𝛿 ∈ ( 0, 1). In this case, the offline data is uninformative. 

The proof of Proposition 1 is deferred to Section C.3. 

Remark 2. The one-hot feature mapping for any offline–online tabular MDP (see Remark 1) satisfies uniform learnability with 𝐼 = |S||A| , 𝐿 = |S| , 𝑉 𝑠 ′ = 𝑒 𝑠 ′ for all 𝑠 ′ ∈ S , and 𝜅 = 1, where 𝑒 𝑠 ′ ∈ R| S | denotes the canonical basis vector corresponding to state 𝑠 ′ in the one-hot representation. Furthermore, since we assume finite state and action spaces in this subsection, any offline–online linear mixture MDP admits a tabular reformulation, under which the one-hot feature mapping of the offline MDP is uniformly learnable. However, uniform learnability is defined with respect to the given linear mixture representation with dimension 𝑑 , rather than the reformulated tabular one. 

Offline Behavior Policy. A coverage condition on the offline behavior policy is also required for the offline data to be informative. Intuitively, even if the feature mapping of the offline MDP is uniformly learnable, the offline behavior policy must visit the specific (𝑠, 𝑎 ) pairs in Definition 4 in order to obtain a nontrivial lower bound on 𝜏 implied by uniform learnability. Inspired by the offline RL literature (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023, Jin et al. 2021, Yin et al. 2022, Uehara and Sun 2021), we focus on the uniform data coverage assumption (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2021, 2023), which is a canonical assumption in offline RL. The discussion of other coverage assumptions, including partial data coverage (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023) or feature-space coverage (Yin et al. 2022), is deferred to Section B.3. Formally, for any behavior policy 𝜋 off , define the marginal state–action probability measure at stage ℎ in the offline MDP as

Design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. Even under uniform learnability and offline data coverage, however, a well-designed choice of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } is necessary to achieve a nontrivial value of 𝜏 . We illustrate this via a simple counterexample. Consider an offline–online tabular MDP embedded into an offline–online linear mixture MDP via Remark 1. In this case, the offline MDP is uniformly learnable with probe vectors chosen as unit vectors. Moreover, under an appropriate design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } and offline behavior policy, one can obtain 𝜏 = Θ (1), as in Remark 2. However, if one sets e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ = 1| S | for all 𝑚, ℎ , the resulting offline design matrix 𝐺 off is not full rank, yielding 𝜏 = 𝜆 𝑀 off regardless of the offline behavior policy. This shows that a well-designed {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } is necessary. To maximize 𝜏 , a natural principle is to select {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } so as to increase the minimum eigenvalue of the offline design matrix 𝐺 off . Adaptive Offline Behavior Policy and Decaying Coverage 𝜏 . Although the analysis above mainly focuses on the regime 𝜏 = Θ (1) under the uniform data coverage assumption (Assumption 2), this assumption can be overly idealized, as it essentially requires the offline behavior policies {𝜋 off  

> 𝑚

}𝑀 off  

> 𝑚 =1

to be designed for exploration. In practice, the offline data may instead be generated by learning algorithms that aim to minimize regret in the offline environment. In this case the behavior policy becomes adaptive, and the visitation distribution gradually concentrates around the optimal policy of the offline MDP rather than exploring the state–action space uniformly. We show that even in this setting the offline data can remain informative under our algorithm O-O UCRL -

VTR . This corresponds to Case 2 of Section 4.2, where the coverage parameter 𝜏 may decay with 𝑀 off .To illustrate this phenomenon, we consider an offline–online tabular bandit instance with 𝐴 arms, where the offline behavior policies follow the UCB1 algorithm (Auer et al. 2002). In this instance, let 𝜇 off (𝑖 ) and

{𝜉 𝑚 }𝑚 ∈ [ 𝑀 off ] are i.i.d. 𝑁 (0, 1) noises. The reward model in the online environment is defined analogously. Let 𝜇 off ,∗ and 𝜇 ∗ denote the expected rewards of the optimal arms in the offline and online environments, respectively; the optimal arms in the two environments may differ. Define

For this reformulation, we assume that Assumption 1 holds, namely that the environment shift is upper bounded by Δ and is known. > 3This condition arises from the high-probability bound on the number of pulls in a bandit model provided by Proposition 3.7 of Han et al. (2024). When Δoff gap decreases, meaning that the expected rewards of different arms in the offline bandit problem become closer, UCB1 explores suboptimal arms more frequently, which leads to a larger effective coverage parameter 𝜏 . As a result, a larger value of Δ can be tolerated while the offline data remains informative. This illustrates the relation between 𝜏 and Δ in Figure 1 when the regime 𝜏 = Θ (1) no longer holds. Note that Δoff gap affects the online phase in two ways. A smaller 

Δoff gap (together with a small Δ) makes the online bandit problem harder due to smaller suboptimality gaps, while it also induces more exploration in the offline phase under UCB1 and leads to a larger 𝜏 , making the offline data more informative. Intuitively, this reveals a somewhat counterintuitive phenomenon: harder online instances may allow the offline data to provide greater benefit. 

## 5. Regret Lower Bound 

We complement our upper bound in Theorem 1 with a nearly matching lower bound when 𝜏 = Θ (1).

Theorem 2. Under environment shift (Assumption 1) and coverage condition (Definition 2), for any 

Δ, 𝐻 ≥ 2, and 𝑑 ≥ 2, for any sufficiently large 𝐾 and 𝑀 off 4 , and any offline–online algorithm, there exists an offline–online linear mixture MDP instance such that, whenever the offline data induces 𝜏 = Θ (1), any algorithm has regret at least

. (23) The proof of Theorem 2 is deferred to Section C.4. The lower bound in Equation (23) exhibits a “min-of-two” structure, mirroring the upper bound in Theorem 1. The lower bound in Eq. (23) matches the upper bound in Eq. (13) in the dependence on 𝑑 and 𝐾 when the online-only term 𝑑𝐻 √𝐾 dominates, corresponding to the regime where the offline data is not informative. The gap in the dependence on 𝐻 is consistent with the linear mixture MDP literature (Zhou et al. 2021, Jia et al. 2020), and can be reduced via variance-aware analysis as in Zhou et al. (2021). When the offline–online term dominates, corresponding to the regime where the offline data is informative, the condition 𝜏 = Θ (1) ensures that 𝜆 min (E[𝐺 off ]) = Θ (𝑀 off ) (see Eq. (59) in Appendix), under which the lower bound matches Eq. (13) in the dependence on 𝐾 , 𝑀 off , and Δ. The dependence on 𝑑 is also nearly matched, up to a gap between 𝑑 32 and √𝑑 in the Δ-dependent term. This gap, together with the gap in the dependence on 𝐻 , stems from the use of self-normalized concentration (Lemma 7) in the upper bound and from measuring offline data through the worst-direction eigenvalue in Definition 2, which is consistent with the offline RL literature (see, e.g., Table 1 of Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023)).              

> 4The requirement that 𝐾 and 𝑀 off are sufficiently large in Theorem 2 arises from asymptotic notations, such as 𝜏 = Θ (1), and is not intrinsic to the result. In particular, it suffices that 𝐾 = Ω (𝑑 2)ad 𝑀 off = Ω (𝑑 2).Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 21

We next discuss the lower bound in the regime 𝜏 = 𝑜 (1) and show that this case leads to a different regret lower bound, as the dependence on 𝑑 in Theorem 2 no longer applies. To illustrate, consider an offline–online bandit problem with 𝐴 arms (see Section A.1) with no environment shift (so 𝑑 = Θ ( 𝐴 )). Suppose that all but one arm are well-covered in the offline data, while the remaining arm is never explored in the offline phase. In this case, we have 𝜏 = 𝑂 

 1

> 𝑀 off



and 𝜆 min (E[𝐺 off ]) = 𝑂 (1), as discussed in Definition 2. Substituting these quantities and Δ = 0 into Eq. (23) would yield a lower bound of order 𝑑𝐻 √𝐾 from Theorem 2, which is incorrect; In this instance, the problem reduces to identifying a single poorly explored arm in the online phase, and the regret need not scale with 𝑑 . This example highlights that, when 𝜏 = 𝑜 (1), the lower bound is governed more by the geometry of the offline data, as captured by 𝜆 min (E[𝐺 off ]) , rather than by the dimension 𝑑 . However, the relation 𝜆 min (E[𝐺 off ]) = Θ (𝑀 off ) no longer holds in this regime. In principle, one can derive lower bounds for 𝜏 = 𝑜 (1) following the same framework as in the proof of Theorem 2, under additional conditions on the offline behavior policy that characterize 𝜆 min (E[𝐺 off ]) . We do not pursue this direction here, as it requires a detailed, instance-dependent characterization of the behavior policy, which is beyond the scope of this paper. 

## 6. Numerical Simulations 

In this section, we further support the results in Section 4 with numerical experiments on a synthetic tabular MDP. In particular, we study the regret dependence on 𝑀 off , the coverage parameter 𝜏 , the environment shift 

Δ, and the choice of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. We also investigate when historical data becomes informative, as characterized by the trade-off between the environment shift Δ and the coverage parameter 𝜏 . See https://github. com/Zhongjun24/Offline_Online_RL for the code base. 

6.1. Baselines and Simulation Setting 

We implement O-O UCRL -VTR under the following two designs for {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }:

• O-O UCRL -VTR (Optimal ): {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } is constructed according to the deterministic design in Section 4.3, noting that the underlying setting is a tabular MDP. 

• O-O UCRL -VTR (Pessimistic ) (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023): Motivated by prior work that applies pessimism-based estimation in the offline phase of offline–online RL (Zheng et al. 2023), we also incorporate a pessimism-based variant for constructing {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } by adapting Algorithm 2 of Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023) in the offline phase. The online phase remains identical to O-O UCRL -VTR .We additionally compare O-O UCRL -VTR against the following baselines: 

• UCRL (Jia et al. 2020): An algorithm specifically designed for online linear mixture MDPs. When no offline data are available, O-O UCRL -VTR reduces to a standard online algorithm and coincides with 

UCRL .

• COMPLETE (Algorithm 2 in Appendix E): This baseline closely follows the COMPLETE approach in Chen et al. (2022) and HUCB1 for bandits (Shivaswamy and Joachims 2012), which assumes Δ = 0

and directly merges offline data into online learning. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 22 10 −2 10 −1 10 0 10 1

Each experiment is repeated over 50 independent runs. Further details on the synthetic tabular MDP, behavior policy, baseline implementations, and experimental settings are provided in Appendix E. 

6.2. Simulation Results Robustness to Environment Shift Δ. We first examine how performance varies with the environment shift Δ. Fig. 2a plots the cumulative regret as Δ increases, with 𝜏 = Θ (1), 𝐾 = 4 × 10 3, and 𝑀 off = 10 5 fixed. As expected, UCRL remains insensitive to Δ since it does not use any historical data. When Δ is small, we see that O-O UCRL -VTR significantly outperforms UCRL , demonstrating how our algorithm is able to effectively leverage offline data. As Δ grows, the regret of O-O UCRL -VTR increases smoothly and approaches that of UCRL , demonstrating the safeguarding property in Theorem 1. When offline data is uninformative, the algorithm gracefully falls back to purely online learning. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 23 

In contrast, methods that directly merge offline and online data without explicit robustness mechanisms, such as COMPLETE , exhibit substantial performance degradation when Δ becomes large. This phenomenon underscores the robustness of O-O UCRL -VTR .

Impact of Offline Sample Size 𝑀 off . We next vary the offline sample size 𝑀 off while fixing Δ = 0.05 ,

𝜏 = Θ (1), and 𝐾 = 4 × 10 3. Fig. 2b shows that once 𝑀 off ≳ 10 3, O-O UCRL -VTR begins to outperform 

UCRL , indicating that the offline data have become informative relative to the online horizon 𝐾 . As 𝑀 off 

increases further, both offline–online approaches improve. This behavior aligns with Corollary 1, which predicts that the offline data become informative once their effective information content is comparable to that of online samples. An interesting observation is that for small 𝑀 off , the regret of COMPLETE first increases and then decreases as 𝑀 off grows, unlike O-O UCRL -VTR . Since COMPLETE ignores the environment shift, when 

𝑀 off is small, increasing 𝑀 off makes the estimator rely more heavily on the offline phase and become increasingly confident in a potentially misspecified model, thereby amplifying bias and increasing regret at 

𝐾 . As 𝑀 off becomes large, COMPLETE effectively commits to the offline model and performs little to no exploration in the online phase. Given that the environment shift Δ = 0.05 is small, this lack of exploration does not incur substantial loss for 𝐾 = 4 × 10 3, leading to a decrease in regret. 

Role of Coverage 𝜏 . We next investigate the effect of the coverage parameter 𝜏 , fixing Δ = 0.05 , 𝐾 =

4 × 10 3, and 𝑀 off = 2 × 10 5. As shown in Fig. 2c, the advantage of O-O UCRL -VTR over UCRL increases as 𝜏 grows. This observation is consistent with the analysis in Section 4. When 𝜏 is small, O-O UCRL -VTR 

does not coincide with UCRL . This is also consistent with the theory in Section 4, since the regret bound depends on the worst-direction coverage, whereas in finite samples, certain well-covered directions may still provide empirical gains. 

Design of the Offline Value Function Estimates {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. Fig. 2d compares two constructions of 

{e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } under Δ = 0.05 , 𝜏 = Θ (1), and 𝑀 off = 10 5. Both O-O UCRL -VTR (Optimal ) and O-O UCRL -VTR 

(Pessimistic ) outperform the purely online baseline UCRL . However, O-O UCRL -VTR (Optimal ) consis-tently attains substantially lower regret than O-O UCRL -VTR (Pessimistic ). This suggests that directly improving the statistical geometry of the offline Gram matrix 𝐺 off can be more effective than adopting a pessimism-driven construction in this setting. The result empirically validates the design principle proposed in Section 4.3. 

Interaction Between Environment Shift and Coverage. Finally, we examine how Δ and 𝜏 interact. Fig. 3 plots regret along trajectories Δ(𝜏 ), with 𝐾 = 4 × 10 3 and 𝑀 off = 10 5 fixed, and all curves calibrated to share the same value of Δ at 𝜏 = 0.01 .When Δ increases linearly with 𝜏 , offline data remain informative even as Δ grows. In contrast, when 

Δ scales quadratically or cubically with 𝜏 , the benefit of offline data rapidly disappears once 𝜏 exceeds Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 24 10 −3 10 −2 10 −1 10 0

0.1. These trends closely mirror the dependence predicted by Corollary 1, which indicates that offline data remain informative when Δ/𝜏 remains controlled. This highlights that the value of offline data is determined by the relative scaling between the environment shift Δ and coverage 𝜏 . In operational settings where a larger environment shift is anticipated (higher Δ), the designer can proactively increase coverage (e.g., by broadening or randomizing the historical behavior policy) to ensure that 𝜏 grows commensurately. However, the coverage parameter 𝜏 need not be optimized to the maximum possible level (e.g., 𝜏 = Θ (1)); As long as the collected coverage 𝜏 scales appropriately with the shift, regret guarantees continue to improve and offline data remain informative. 

## 7. Conclusions 

In this work, we study offline–online learning for linear mixture MDPs under environment shift and an uncontrollable offline behavior policy. We develop an algorithm O-O UCRL -VTR for this setting, together with a regret upper bound and nearly matching lower bounds, and characterize when offline data is informa-tive. Our results show that offline data is informative when the environment shift Δ is small and the coverage parameter 𝜏 is large, and that these two factors can compensate for each other. In particular, offline data can remain informative even under non-negligible environment shift, as long as Δ scales linearly with 𝜏 . We further disentangle the factors that determine 𝜏 , including the feature mapping of the linear mixture MDP, the offline behavior policy, and the design of offline value function estimates {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }.Several directions for future work arise from our results. One natural question is whether the interplay between environment shift and offline behavior policy extends to broader function approximation settings in RL, such as offline–online learning with low-dimensional representations. Another direction is to go beyond the scalar quantity 𝜏 and study the full spectrum of the offline design matrix, which may provide a more refined characterization of offline data informativeness and potentially unify the lower bounds under different coverage regimes. Appendix A: Comparisons to Settings in the Literature 

The offline–online linear mixture MDP setting studied in this paper subsumes several canonical models of interest, including offline–online tabular and linear bandits, offline–online tabular MDPs, and offline linear mixture MDPs. In this section, we show how these three settings can be embedded into our offline–online linear mixture MDP framework, and we compare our main result in Theorem 1 with the corresponding guarantees established for each setting in the literature. 

A.1. Offline–Online Tabular and Linear Bandits (Cheung and Lyu 2024, Zhang et al. 2025a) 

We start by examining the connection between Theorem 1 and prior work on offline–online tabular ban-dits (Cheung and Lyu 2024) and offline–online linear bandits (Zhang et al. 2025a). Since tabular bandits can be expressed as linear bandits through one-hot mapping, similar to Remark 1, both models can be viewed as special cases of the offline–online linear mixture MDP framework. We show that (i) the environment shift assumption in Assumption 1 is no stronger than those adopted in (Cheung and Lyu 2024, Zhang et al. 2025a); (ii) how the coverage assumption in Definition 2 connects with the coverage assumptions in these literature; and (iii) in benign regimes, the regret bound in Theorem 1 matches the order of the regret guar-antee in Cheung and Lyu (2024). Note that Zhang et al. (2025a) consider a contextual model, and therefore the regret guarantees are not directly comparable. 

A.1.1. Comparison of Environment Shift Assumptions In stochastic tabular bandits with 𝐴 arms (Cheung and Lyu 2024), the environment shift is characterized by an arm-wise reward discrepancy: for any arm 𝑎 ,

|𝜇 (𝑎 ) − 𝜇 off (𝑎 )| ≤ Δ1 (24) where 𝜇 off (𝑎 ) and 𝜇 (𝑎 ) denote the expected rewards of arm 𝑎 in the offline and online environments, respectively. Similarly, in stochastic linear bandits (Zhang et al. 2025a), the expected reward under action 

𝑎 ∈ R𝑑 is modeled as 𝜇 (𝑎 ) = ⟨𝜃 ∗

> 𝐵

, 𝑎 ⟩ for the online environment, and 𝜇 off (𝑎 ) = ⟨𝜃 off ,∗ 

> 𝐵

, 𝑎 ⟩ for the offline environment. The environment shift is then quantified by: 

∥𝜃 off ,∗ 

> 𝐵

− 𝜃 ∗ 

> 𝐵

(25) into offline–online linear mixture MDPs, which directly induces the relationship between Eq. (24) in a tabular bandit model and the environment shift Δ in Assumption 1. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 32 

We now describe a mapping between linear bandits and linear mixture MDPs. For simplicity, we consider a stochastic Bernoulli offline–online linear bandit. Given an offline–online linear bandit instance with parameters 𝜃 off ,∗ 

> 𝐵

and 𝜃 ∗

> 𝐵

, we construct an offline–online linear mixture MDP as follows. The state space consists of three states {𝑠 1, 𝑠 2, 𝑠 3}, and the episode length is 𝐻 = 2. At stage ℎ = 1, the initial state is always 𝑠 1

and the reward is zero. Upon taking action 𝑎 , the system transitions from 𝑠 1 to 𝑠 2 with probability ⟨𝜃 off ,∗ 

> 𝐵

, 𝑎 ⟩

in the offline environment and ⟨𝜃 ∗

> 𝐵

, 𝑎 ⟩ in the online environment. With the remaining probability, the system transitions to 𝑠 3. At stage ℎ = 2, the reward at 𝑠 2 is 1, while the reward at 𝑠 3 is 0.This offline–online linear mixture MDP satisfies all assumptions in the main text. In particular, the reward functions 𝑟 off and 𝑟 are known, and the induced stochastic process is equivalent to that of the original linear bandit problem. As a result, we obtain the following relationship between the environment shifts. 

Proposition 3. Consider a stochastic offline–online linear bandit problem for which Eq. (25) holds. Let 

(𝑃 off , 𝑟 ) and (𝑃, 𝑟 ) be the equivalent offline and online linear mixture MDPs constructed above, with param-eter vectors 𝜃 off ,∗ and 𝜃 ∗. We define Δ(Δ2) as the induced environment shift bound under the constructed offline–online linear mixture MDP. Then Assumption 1 holds with Δ(Δ2) ≤ Δ2.

Proof. This follows immediately from the construction above, under which the linear mixture MDP parameters coincide with the corresponding linear bandit parameters. □

Similarly, for any offline–online tabular bandit problem satisfying Eq. (24), there exists an equivalent offline–online linear mixture MDP representation. Let Δ(Δ1) denote the induced environment shift bound under the resulting offline–online linear mixture MDP. We then directly obtain that Δ(Δ1) ≤ Δ2 (Δ1) ≤ √𝐴 Δ1,

where the second inequality is from Eq. (26). Consequently, for any offline–online tabular bandit problem under Eq. (24), or offline–online linear bandit problem under Eq. (25), Theorem 1 applies.

A.2. Offline–Online Tabular MDPs (Chen et al. 2022) 

By Remark 1, any offline–online tabular MDP can be embedded into the offline–online linear mixture MDP framework. Analogous to Section A.1, we examine the relation between Theorem 1 and prior work on offline–online tabular MDPs (Chen et al. 2022), focusing on the respective environment shift assumptions, coverage assumptions, and regret guarantees. Unlike the regret bound in Chen et al. (2022), which incurs linear dependence on the environment shift in all regimes, the bound in Theorem 1 avoids this dependence when the offline data are uninformative, typically when the environment shift is large, due to the safeguard structure discussed in Section 4.2.

A.3. Offline Linear Mixture MDPs (Nguyen-Tang et al. 2023) 

We finally compare Theorem 1 with the suboptimality guarantees for offline linear mixture MDPs studied in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023). When 𝐾 = 1 and Δ = 0, our regret bound reduces to a purely offline suboptimality bound; see Section B.5 for details. Since this regime involves no environment shift, we focus on comparing the respective coverage assumptions and suboptimality gap guarantees in Theorem 1 and Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023). As in Section 4.3, for clarity of exposition, we assume in this section that the state and action spaces are finite, so all probability measures are treated as probability mass functions. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 37    

> A.3.1. Comparison of Coverage Assumptions and the Induced 𝜏

Although the primary contribution of Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023) is to establish suboptimality guarantees under a partial coverage assumption (Assumptions 4.1 and 4.2 in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023)), the uniform data coverage assumption, Assump-tion 2, as discussed in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023), is also sufficient to obtain their suboptimality results for linear mixture MDPs (Theorem 7 and Theorem 9 in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023)). Under the stochastic construction of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } in Definition 5 and the uniform learnability assumption (Definition 4), the uniform data coverage condition in Assumption 2 implies that 𝜏 = Θ (1). This argument is formalized in Corollary 2 in Section 4.3. We defer the discussion of the partial coverage assumptions to Section B.3.2 and show that these assumptions are not applicable for the offline–online learning setting considered here.   

> A.3.2. Comparison of Suboptimality Gap

With discussions in Section A.3.1, we directly obtain the fol-lowing corollary. 

Corollary 5. Under Definition 5, Definition 4 and Assumption 2, when 𝐾 = 1 and Δ = 0, we have 

𝜏 = Θ (1), and the regret bound in Theorem 1 reduces to an offline suboptimality gap of order ˜𝑂 ( 1√𝑀 off ).

Corollary 5 follows directly from combining Corollary 2 with Theorem 1. The ˜𝑂 ( 1√𝑀 off ) rate matches the rate obtained in Theorems 7 and Theorem 9 of Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023), up to logarithmic factors, and is statistically optimal. This shows that, in the purely offline regime, our bound is sharp. 

Appendix B: Omitted Discussion from Section 2 to Section 4 

In this section, we supplement Appendix A with additional discussions beyond comparisons to existing literature. Specifically, we show (i) the environment shift Δ and the resulting discrepancy between offline and online value functions are nearly of the same order (Section B.1); (ii) the necessity of the assumption that Δ

is known in Assumption 1 (Section B.2); (iii) a comparison of Definition 2 and other coverage assumptions in the literature (Section B.3); (iv) Eq. (16) is neither convex nor concave in general (Section B.4); and (v) the technical details omitted in translating Theorem 1 into a suboptimality gap bound for comparison with Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023) in Section A.3 (Section B.5).  

> B.1. Environment Shift in terms of Value Functions

We start off by studying Assumption 1 from a value function perspective. Although one could alternatively define the environment shift Δ directly in terms of value function discrepancies between the offline and online MDPs, we show that Assumption 1 already implies an explicit bound on the difference between value functions induced by the same policy in the offline and online environments. Moreover, there exist instances in which Δ exactly matches this value function gap. We therefore retain the parameter-space definition of Δ

in Assumption 1, which more naturally reflects the model geometry and supports the regret analysis. Denote 𝑉 off , 𝜋 ℎ as the value function at stage ℎ under policy 𝜋 in the offline MDP, defined analogously to 

𝑉 𝜋 ℎ in the online MDP in Eq. (2). B.2. Impossibility of Unknown Δ

We briefly discuss the offline–online linear mixture MDP setting in which no explicit upper bound on 

∥𝜃 ∗ − 𝜃 off ,∗ ∥2 (or any equivalent measure of environment shift) is given, i.e., Δ is unknown. The main takeaway is that, without knowing Δ, any algorithm that achieves a regret rate strictly better than √𝐾 in the no-shift case 𝜃 ∗ = 𝜃 off ,∗ must necessarily suffer a √𝐾 -order regret on some shifted instance. Thus, knowledge of an upper bound on Δ is essential for obtaining a worst-case guarantee that uniformly improves upon the online-only rate while remaining strictly better in the no-shift regime. 

Proposition 7. Consider the class of offline–online linear mixture MDP instances for which all assump-tions in the main text hold except Assumption 1, i.e., the environment shift is not assumed to be bounded by a known constant. Suppose an algorithm satisfies Regret (𝐾 ) = 𝑜 (√𝐾 ) over the instance class when 𝜃 ∗ = 𝜃 off ,∗,then there exists another instance in the same class with 𝜃 ∗ ≠ 𝜃 off ,∗ for which the algorithm incurs regret 

Regret (𝐾 ) > Ω(√𝐾 ).

We omit the proof of Proposition 7, as Cheung and Lyu (2024) provides an explicit hard instance construction in the tabular bandit setting (see Proposition 3.1 in Cheung and Lyu (2024)). By the equivalence discussed in Section A.1.1, such instances can be embedded as a special case of offline–online linear mixture MDPs. 

B.3. Comparison of Definition 2 and Assumption 2 with Other Coverage Assumptions B.3.1. Comparison with Feature Coverage Assumptions Beyond the uniform data coverage assumption (Assumption 2) discussed in Section 4.3, feature coverage assumptions, often formulated in a form similar to Definition 2, are also widely adopted in offline RL models with linear structure. In this subsection, we compare Definition 2 with other feature coverage assumptions used in related models in the literature. 

Comparison with Yin et al. (2022). Although Yin et al. (2022) study linear MDPs, whose feature space differs from that of linear mixture MDPs, the underlying coverage assumption is essentially analogous to Definition 2. Specifically, they assume that, with high probability, 𝜆 min 

Í𝑀 off  

> 𝑚 =1

𝜙 𝑚 𝜙 ⊤

> 𝑚



≥ 𝜏𝑀 off , where 𝜙 𝑚 

denotes the feature vector associated with the 𝑚 -th offline sample (see Assumption 2.2 and Lemma H.5 in Yin et al. (2022)). This condition ensures that the empirical design matrix grows linearly in the number of offline samples in every direction in the feature space. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 40 

In our linear mixture MDP setting, the role of 𝜙 𝑚 is played by the induced feature 𝜙 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 )e𝑉 . While linear MDPs do not require the design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } due to their structural properties, both Yin et al. (2022) and Definition 2 formulate coverage in terms of a lower bound on the minimum eigenvalue of an empirical design matrix. However, the scope of Definition 2 is strictly broader. The assumption in Yin et al. (2022) enforces a non-trivial bound 𝜏 = Θ (1), corresponding to the ideal case discussed in Section 4.2. In con-trast, Definition 2 accommodates more general regimes in which 𝜏 may decay as 𝑀 off increases (Case 2 in Section 4.2). 

B.3.2. Comparison with Partial Data Coverage Assumptions Unlike in offline RL, canonical partial data coverage assumptions may not be directly applicable in the offline–online setting due to the presence of environment shift. In this subsection we illustrate this issue using the partial data coverage assumption in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2021) (Assumption 4.1 therein). Concretely, the partial data coverage assumption in Nguyen-Tang et al. (2021) states that for any (ℎ, 𝑠, 𝑎 )

tuple, 𝑑 𝜋 off ,∗ 

> ℎ

(𝑠, 𝑎 ) > 0 ⇒ 𝑑 𝜋 off  

> ℎ

(𝑠, 𝑎 ) > 0, where 𝜋 off ,∗ denotes an optimal policy of the offline MDP, and 

𝑑 𝜋 off ,∗ 

> ℎ

denotes the marginal state–action probability measure under 𝜋 off ,∗, defined analogously to 𝑑 𝜋 off 

> ℎ

in Section 4.3. We now present a simple counterexample showing that, for any 𝑀 off , 𝐾 , and Δ > 0, this assumption is not sufficient to guarantee that the offline data is informative according to Definition 3. Consider an offline–online tabular bandit with three arms. Suppose the mean rewards of the arms satisfy 

𝜇 (1) = 0.5, 𝜇 off (1) = 0.5, 𝜇 (2) = 0.5 − 1

√𝐾 , 𝜇 off (2) = 0.5 − 1

√𝐾 , 𝜇 (3) = 0.5 − 14 Δ, 𝜇 off (3) = 0.5 + 14 Δ,

where 𝜇 and 𝜇 off follow the definitions in Section A.1. As discussed in Proposition 3, this instance satisfies Assumption 1. In the offline environment, arm 3 is optimal. Suppose the offline behavior policy always pulls arm 3.Then the partial coverage assumption above is satisfied, since the optimal offline policy also selects arm 3.However, in the online environment arm 1 is optimal. Since the offline data contains no information about arm 1 and arm 2, the learner must explore arm 2 during the online phase. Consequently, the regret remains of order √𝐾 regardless of the value of 𝑀 off , and the offline data is therefore not informative. This completes the argument, since under the uniform data coverage assumption (Assumption 2), the offline data can be informative for appropriate values of 𝑀 off , 𝐾 , and Δ > 0, as discussed in Section A.1. If the partial coverage assumption were instead defined with respect to the optimal policy of the online environment, one might obtain positive guarantees when the offline and online MDPs share the same state and action spaces. However, in general offline–online settings the two environments may differ substantially, and such a definition may not even be well-defined. We leave the investigation of such coverage conditions for future work. Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 41 

B.4. Discussion on the Convexity of Eq. (16) 

In this section we provide a counterexample showing that, in general, Eq. (16) is neither concave nor convex with respect to 𝑉 . Consequently, one cannot expect a closed-form solution or to leverage convex optimization algorithms to solve it in general. Before presenting the proof of Theorem 1, we introduce a filtration that will play a central role in the analysis. The filtration jointly accounts for the offline and online phases. To this end, we introduce a unified indexing scheme that orders all samples from both phases. Specifically, consider the collection of offline indices Ioff := {( 𝑚, ℎ ) : 𝑚 = 1, . . . , 𝑀 off ; ℎ = 1, . . . , 𝐻 }, and the collection of online indices Ion := {( 𝑘, ℎ ) :

𝑘 = 1, . . . , 𝐾 ; ℎ = 1, . . . , 𝐻 }. We consider the unique one-one mapping 𝑡 ←→ ( 𝑖 𝑡 , ℎ 𝑡 ) ∈ I off ∪ I on that orders all samples, such that all offline indices in Ioff precede those in Ion . Note that the mapping is unique as (i) within each trajectory, samples are indexed by ℎ = 1, . . . , 𝐻 ; (ii) online trajectories are indexed by 

𝑘 = 1, . . . , 𝐾 ; and (iii) offline trajectories are indexed by 𝑚 = 1, . . . , 𝑀 off , which induces a unique order due to the adaptive behavior policy. For each time index 𝑡 , we define the filtration F𝑡 as follows. 

• If 𝑡 corresponds to an offline index (𝑚, ℎ ) ∈ I off , then F𝑡 is the 𝜎 -algebra generated by the offline observations up to and including (𝑚, ℎ ), that is,

We next bound the gap between the optimistic value estimates produced by the algorithm and the true value functions induced by the executed policy 𝜋 𝑘 . The analysis follows a standard telescoping argument along the trajectory within an episode: the gap at stage ℎ is related to the gap at stage ℎ + 1, plus a martingale difference term accounting for the randomness of the next state. The remaining term is controlled by the uncertainty in the transition parameter, which is quantified through the confidence radii associated with the sets C𝑘 and B𝑘 .

Lemma 10. Let 𝐴, 𝐵 ∈ R𝑑 ×𝑑 be symmetric and positive definite matrices. If 𝐴 ⪰ 𝐵 ≻ 0, that is, 𝐴 − 𝐵 is positive semidefinite, then 𝐵 −1 ⪰ 𝐴 −1.

Lemma 14. Let 𝐴 ∈ R𝑑 ×𝑑 be a random positive semidefinite matrix. Then E[𝜆 min ( 𝐴 )] ≤ 𝜆 min (E[ 𝐴 ]) .

Baseline Algorithms. In addition to the baselines introduced in the main text, in this section we include comparisons between O-O UCRL -VTR and two tabular MDP algorithms, DP-LSVI and UCBVI .

• DP-LSVI (Algorithm 2 in Chen et al. (2022)): The offline–online tabular MDP algorithm proposed by Chen et al. (2022). In contrast to Algorithm 1, DP-LSVI can perform worse than a purely online method when environment shift Δ is large. 

• UCBVI (Azar et al. 2017): A standard purely online algorithm for tabular MDPs. The comparison between O-O UCRL -VTR and the two tabular MDP algorithms is presented in Figure 4. The implementations of UCRL , DP-LSVI , UCBVI , O-O UCRL -VTR (Optimal ), and O-O UCRL -VTR 

(Pessimistic ) are straightforward. For implementation details of UCRL , DP-LSVI , UCBVI , and the offline phase of O-O UCRL -VTR (Pessimistic ), we refer the reader to Jia et al. (2020), Chen et al. (2022), Azar et al. (2017), and Nguyen-Tang et al. (2023), respectively. Following the spirit of the COMPLETE 

algorithm proposed in Chen et al. (2022), we present the full description of our COMPLETE baseline in Algorithm 2. Algorithm 2 closely mirrors Algorithm 1, with only two differences: (i) it does not employ the “min-of-two” safeguard (since assuming no environment shift), and (ii) the confidence set is defined as

E.2. MDP Setting and Behavior Policy 

We consider a synthetic episodic MDP with |S| = 5, |A| = 10 , 𝐻 = 3, and 𝐵 = 2. We follow the setting 

𝜆 = 𝐻 2𝑑 as in the main text. Under the standard embedding of tabular MDPs into linear mixture MDPs (Remark 1), the feature dimension is 𝑑 = |S| 2 |A| = 250 . For each (𝑠, 𝑎 ), the reward 𝑟 (𝑠, 𝑎 ) is sampled independently from the uniform distribution Unif (0, 1).

Online and Offline Transition Kernel. In the online transition kernel 𝑃 , each 𝑃 (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) is sampled from a Dirichlet distribution with parameter (1, 1, . . . , 1) over the probability simplex, yielding uniform density over transitions. The offline transition kernel 𝑃 off is constructed to maximize the discrepancy between the offline and online environments subject to the budget Δ. Given a fixed online transition kernel 𝑃 and reward function 𝑟 , we first compute, for each state 𝑠 ∈ S , the value 𝑉 (𝑠 ) induced by the worst policy under the online transition kernel 𝑃 . For each state–action pair (𝑠, 𝑎 ), we then solve the following constrained optimization problem: 

𝑃 off (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) = arg min  

> 𝑃 off (· | 𝑠,𝑎 )

E𝑠 ′∼𝑃 off (· | 𝑠,𝑎 ) [𝑉 (𝑠 ′)] s.t. ∥𝑃 off (𝑠, 𝑎 ) − 𝑃 (𝑠, 𝑎 )∥ 1 ≤ Δ. (70) Intuitively, states with smaller values of 𝑉 (𝑠 ) are regarded as worse under the online kernel 𝑃 . Under constraint Δ, the construction Eq. (70) shifts probability mass of 𝑃 off (· | 𝑠, 𝑎 ) toward such states for every 

(𝑠, 𝑎 ). Note that, although this procedure yields a substantially shifted offline environment, it does not necessarily correspond to the most adversarial design of 𝑃 off .Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 71

Behavior Policy and Control of 𝜏 . In all experiments, the offline behavior policy is the random policy. In Fig. 2a, 2b, and 2d, where 𝜏 = Θ (1), we adopt the designs of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } described in Section 6 for O-O UCRL -

VTR (Optimal ) and O-O UCRL -VTR (Pessimistic ). In Fig. 2c and Fig. 3, where we vary 𝜏 , the behavior policy is fixed and 𝜏 is controlled through the design of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ }. Specifically, let S = {𝑠 1, 𝑠 2, 𝑠 3, 𝑠 4, 𝑠 5}. For any target value of 𝜏 , whenever the state at (𝑚, ℎ ) is 𝑠 1 or 𝑠 2, we set P(e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ = 0) = 1 − 𝜏, and with probability 

𝜏 , we apply the designs of {e𝑉 𝑚,ℎ } described in Section 6. 

Additional Simulation Results Including DP-LSVI and UCBVI . We conclude this section by presenting addi-tional simulation results that include comparisons with DP-LSVI and UCBVI .Zhang and Sinclair: Offline–Online Liner Mixture MDPs under Environment Shift 72 10 −2 10 −1 10 0 10 1

(b) Regret (𝐾 ) versus 𝑀 off .UCRL COMPLETE DP–LSVI UCBVI O–O UCRL–VLR (Optimal)  

> Figure 4 Supplementary results to Figure 2, including DP-LSVI and UCBVI. The setting in Figure 4a matches that of Figure 2a, and the setting in Figure 4b matches that of Figure 2b.

As shown in Fig. 4, UCRL and UCBVI remain insensitive to Δ since it does not use any offline data. 

DP-LSVI and UCBVI outperform UCRL , O-O UCRL -VTR , and COMPLETE across many regimes. This is expected, as DP-LSVI and UCBVI are designed for tabular settings, whereas O-O UCRL -VTR is developed for the more general linear mixture MDP framework. However, in certain regimes of 𝑀 off and Δ (e.g., large Δ in Figure 4a and large 𝑀 off in Figure 4b), O-OUCRL -VTR can outperform UCBVI and DP-LSVI , as DP-LSVI lacks a safeguard mechanism and UCBVI does not utilize the offline data. This suggests that a well-designed integration of offline data can compensate for, and in some regimes even overcome, the dimensional disadvantage inherent in the linear mixture formulation, thereby validating the effectiveness of O-O UCRL -VTR .
